Oath Without Time Specifier: Present Ownership Only
Explanation of the ruling when 'yawma'idhin' is omitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath regarding slaves when no future time is specified and explain the linguistic basis for the default present interpretation.

Core Idea
Without 'yawma'idhin', an oath on slaves applies only to current ownership.

When a person swears an oath like 'every slave of mine is free' without adding a time qualifier (e.g., 'tomorrow'), a key question arises: which slaves are affected? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that the oath applies only to slaves owned at the moment of utterance. The reasoning draws on linguistic principles, customary usage, and legal context. Understanding this ruling clarifies the default scope of such oaths and distinguishes between present and future ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is an oath: 'every slave of mine is free.' The ruling is that without the word 'yawma'idhin', only slaves owned at the time of speaking are emancipated.
The word 'yawma'idhin' means 'that day' and specifies a future time.
2
Locate the Linguistic Proof
The author argues that the possessive 'li' (mine) indicates present ownership because the 'lam' is for specificity (ikhtisas), and the participle 'mamluk' (owned) is understood as current at the time of speaking.
The Arabic grammar rule: active/passive participles denote the present state.
3
Apply Customary and Legal Evidence
Customarily, saying 'he eats' refers to now. Legally, saying 'I bear witness' binds immediately. Thus, the default is present.
These reinforce that unrestricted speech points to the current moment.
4
Examine the Exception for Intent
If the speaker intended future slaves, his intention is not accepted in court for excluding current slaves, but it binds him between himself and Allah as an acknowledgment.
Similar to the divorce case: the apparent wording governs externally.
5
Extend to Similar Phrasing
The ruling applies equally to 'every slave I own at this hour' (as-sa'ah) and whether the emancipation is immediate or suspended, or with any condition particle.
The phrase 'as-sa'ah' means the present moment in common usage, not the astrological hour.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو لم يقل يومئذ لا أي لا يعتق ما يملكه بعده وإنما يعتق من وقت التكلم لأن قوله كل مملوك لي يختص بالحال والجزاء حرية المملوك في الحال يتعلق في الحال بمملوك أي المملوك في الحال حريته هي الجزاء، وإنما كانت للحال لأن المختار في الوصف من اسم الفاعل والمفعول أن معناه قائم حال التكلم بمن نسب إليه على وجه قيامه به أو وقوعه عليه واللام للاختصاص، فلو لم يكن في ملكه شيء يوم حلف كان اليمين لغواً. ولا فرق بين كون العتق معلقاً كما في الكتاب أو منجزاً، أو سواء قدم الشرط أو أخره، وسواء كان التعليق بـ (إن) كما في الكتاب أو بغيرها كإذا دخلت أو إذا ما أو متی أو متى ما . وقوله «لي» ليس بقيد لأنه لو قال كل مملوك أملكه فهو حر ولا نية له فإنه لما كان في حلف فقط لأن صيغة «افعل» وإن كانت تستعمل للحال والاستقبال لكن عند الإطلاق يراد به الحال عرفاً وشرعاً ولغة أما العرف فإن من قال فلان يأكل أو يشرب أو يفعل كذا يريد به الحال. ويقول الرجل ما أملك ألف درهم ويريد به الحال وأما الشرع فإن من قال أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله يكون مؤمناً، ولو قال أشهد أن لفلان علي فلان كذا كان شاهداً. وأما اللغة فإن هذه الصيغة موضوعة للحال على طريق الأصالة لأنه ليس للحال صيغة أخرى وللاستقبال سين وسوف فكانت الحال أصلاً فيها والاستقبال دخيلاً، فعند الاطلاق ينصرف إلى الحال ولو قال عنيت به ما استقبل ملکه عتق ما ملكه للحال وما استحدث الملك فيه لما ذكرنا أن ظاهرها للحال وبنيته يصرفه عن ظاهره فلا يصدق فيه ويصدق في قوله أردت ما يحدث ملكي فيه في المستقبل فيعتق عليه بإقراره كما إذا قال زينب طالق وله امرأة معروفة بهذا الاسم ثم قال لي امرأة أخرى بهذا الاسم عنيتها طلقت المعروفة بظاهر اللفظ والمجهولة باعترافه كذا ها هنا. وكذا لو قال كل مملوك أملكه الساعة فهو حر إن هذا يقع على ما في ملكه وقت اليمين ولا يعتق ما يستفيده بعد ذلك إلا أن يكون نوى ذلك فيلزمه ما نوى لأن المراد من الساعة المذكورة هي الساعة المعروفة عند الناس وهي الحال لا الساعة الزمانية التي يذكرها المنجمون فيتناول هذا الكلام من كان في ملكه وقت التكلم لا من يستفيده من بعد، فإن قال أردت به من أستفيده في هذه الساعة الزمانية يصدق فيه لأن اللفظ يحتمله وفيه تشديد على نفسه ولكن لا يصدق في صرف اللفظ عمن يكون في ملكه للحال، وسواء أطلق أو علق بشرط قدم الشرط أو أخره؛ كذا في البدائع .
His saying: (And if he did not say 'yawma'idhin', then no, i.e., what he owns afterwards is not emancipated, rather only from the time of speaking is emancipated, because his saying 'every slave of mine' is specific to the present, and the recompense is the freedom of the slave in the present; it relates in the present to a slave, meaning the slave in the present, his freedom is the recompense. It is only for the present because the chosen view regarding the description from active and passive participles is that its meaning is established at the time of speaking to the one it is attributed to, in the manner of its existence or occurrence upon him, and the 'lam' indicates specificity. So if he did not own anything on the day he swore, the oath is idle. There is no difference between whether the emancipation is suspended as in the book or immediate, nor whether he placed the condition first or last, nor whether the suspension is with 'in' as in the book or with other words like 'idha dakhaltu' or 'idha ma' or 'mata' or 'mata ma'. And his saying 'li' is not a restriction, because if he said 'every slave I own is free' and he has no intention, then it is only for the oath because the form 'af'al' although used for present and future, when unrestricted, means the present customarily, legally, and linguistically. Customarily, if someone says 'so-and-so eats or drinks or does such-and-such', it means the present. A man says 'I do not own a thousand dirhams' and means the present. Legally, if someone says 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah', he becomes a believer; if he says 'I bear witness that so-and-so owes me such-and-such', he is a witness. Linguistically, this form is placed for the present as the original, because there is no other form for the present, and for the future there are 'sa' and 'sawfa', so the present is the original and the future is incidental. Therefore, when unrestricted, it refers to the present. If he said 'I meant what I will own in the future', then what he owns at the present is emancipated, and what he acquires ownership of later is not, because the apparent meaning is for the present, and his intention diverts it from its apparent meaning, so he is not believed [in court] regarding that, but he is believed in his saying 'I intended what I will acquire in the future', so it becomes effective as an acknowledgment, just as if he said 'Zaynab is divorced' and he has a wife known by that name, then said 'I have another wife by that name, I meant her', then the known one is divorced by the apparent wording and the unknown one by his admission. Similarly here. And likewise if he said 'every slave I own at this hour is free', then this applies to what is in his ownership at the time of the oath, and what he acquires afterwards is not emancipated unless he intended that, in which case it binds him as he intended, because what is meant by 'al-sa'ah' (the hour) is the well-known hour among people, i.e., the present, not the temporal hour that astrologers mention. So it includes whoever is in his ownership at the time of speaking, not those he acquires later. If he said 'I meant by it those I will acquire in this temporal hour', he is believed regarding it because the wording can bear it, and there is a restriction on himself, but he is not believed in diverting the wording from those who are in his ownership at present. Whether it is absolute or suspended with a condition, whether he placed the condition first or last; thus in al-Badai'.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when the word 'يومئذ' is omitted from the oath 'كل مملوك لي حر'. In that case, the oath applies only to slaves owned at the moment of speaking, not to future acquisitions. The reasoning is linguistic: the present tense verb or possessive construction ('لي') indicates present ownership. The author supports this with customary, legal, and linguistic evidence. The rule applies whether the emancipation is immediate or suspended, and regardless of the particle used for suspension. Even if the speaker intends future ownership, that intention is not accepted in court to exclude current slaves, but it is accepted between him and Allah. The same applies to 'كل مملوك أملكه الساعة' where 'الساعة' means the present moment. The student should note the Hanafi principle that the present tense is the default for such formulations.

Key Terms
لي (li): Possessive preposition meaning 'belonging to me'; in this context, it indicates ownership at the time of utterance.
الساعة (al-sa'ah): In this context, 'the hour' refers to the present moment in common usage, not a specific temporal hour.
يومئذ (yawma'idhin): 'That day' used to specify a future time for the oath's application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'كل مملوك لي حر' without specifying a time, which slaves are emancipated?
A) All slaves he ever owns.
B) Only the slaves he owns at the time of the oath.
C) Slaves he will own in the future.
D) Slaves he owned in the past.

Key Takeaway
Without a future time qualifier, an oath on owned property applies only to what is owned at the moment of utterance.

Scope of 'Every Slave I Purchase' in Conditional Emancipation
Ruling on conditional emancipation with purchase phrasing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timing of emancipation when the oath uses 'every slave I purchase' and explain how the condition's order affects which slaves are included.

Core Idea
In conditional emancipation, 'every slave I purchase' only frees slaves bought after the condition is met.

When a person makes a conditional oath of emancipation using the phrase 'every slave I purchase is free,' the key question is whether the emancipation applies to slaves bought before the condition or only after. The Hanafi school, based on linguistic analysis and customary usage, holds that the phrase refers strictly to future acquisitions. This lesson explains the ruling and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its phrasing
The passage begins with 'ولو قدم الشرط فقال إن كلمت فلاناً أو إذا كلمت فلاناً أو إذا جاء غد فكل مملوك اشتريه فهو حر'. Note that the condition uses 'اشتريه' (I purchase), which is a present tense verb indicating future acquisition.
The word 'اشتريه' is in the mudari' form, which defaults to future when used in conditional clauses.
2
Determine the temporal scope
The ruling states that 'فهذا على ما يشتريه بعد الكلام لا قبله'. This means the emancipation applies only to slaves purchased after the condition is fulfilled (e.g., after speaking or after tomorrow comes). Slaves purchased before the condition are not included.
Even if he had previously purchased slaves, they are not freed by this oath.
3
Apply the same logic to similar wordings
The passage extends the rule to phrases like 'كل مملوك اشتريه إذا دخلت الدار' or 'إذا قدم فلان'. In all such cases, emancipation applies only to slaves purchased after the action sworn upon.
The exception is if the speaker explicitly intends to include past purchases, but the default is future.
4
Note the absence of difference in conditional particles
The author states there is no difference between using 'إن' or 'إذا' or other particles. The ruling remains consistent: the phrase 'اشتريه' limits the emancipation to future acquisitions.
This reinforces the Hanafi principle that the word 'purchase' in this context is unambiguous.

ولو قدم الشرط فقال إن كلمت فلاناً أو إذا كلمت فلاناً أو إذا جاء غد فكل مملوك اشتريه فهو حر فهذا على ما يشتريه بعد الكلام لا قبله حتى لو كان اشترى مماليك قبل الكلام ثم كلم لا يعتق واحد منهم وما اشتراه بعده يعتق ولو قال كل مملوك اشتريه إذا دخلت الدار فهو حر، أو قال إن قدم فلان فهذا على ما يشتري بعد الفعل الذي حلف عليه ولا يعتق ما اشترى قبل ذلك إلا أن يعينهم . يوم كان في ملکه ملکه قوله : (ولو لم يقل يومئذ (لا أي لا يعتق ما يملكه بعده وإنما يعتق من وقت التكلم لأن قوله كل مملوك لي يختص بالحال والجزاء حرية المملوك في الحال يتعلق في الحال بمملوك أي المملوك في الحال حريته هي الجزاء، وإنما كانت للحال لأن المختار في الوصف من اسم الفاعل والمفعول أن معناه قائم حال التكلم بمن نسب إليه على وجه قيامه به أو وقوعه عليه واللام للاختصاص، فلو لم يكن في ملكه شيء يوم حلف كان اليمين لغواً. ولا فرق بين كون العتق معلقاً كما في الكتاب أو منجزاً، أو سواء قدم الشرط أو أخره، وسواء كان التعليق بـ (إن) كما في الكتاب أو بغيرها كإذا دخلت أو إذا ما أو متی أو متى ما . وقوله «لي» ليس بقيد لأنه لو قال كل مملوك أملكه فهو حر ولا نية له فإنه لما كان في حلف فقط لأن صيغة «افعل» وإن كانت تستعمل للحال والاستقبال لكن عند الإطلاق يراد به الحال عرفاً وشرعاً ولغة أما العرف فإن من قال فلان يأكل أو يشرب أو يفعل كذا يريد به الحال. ويقول الرجل ما أملك ألف درهم ويريد به الحال وأما الشرع فإن من قال أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله يكون مؤمناً، ولو قال أشهد أن لفلان علي فلان كذا كان شاهداً. وأما اللغة فإن هذه الصيغة موضوعة للحال على طريق الأصالة لأنه ليس للحال صيغة أخرى وللاستقبال سين وسوف فكانت الحال أصلاً فيها والاستقبال دخيلاً، فعند الاطلاق ينصرف إلى الحال ولو قال عنيت به ما استقبل ملکه عتق ما ملكه للحال وما استحدث الملك فيه لما ذكرنا أن ظاهرها للحال وبنيته يصرفه عن ظاهره فلا يصدق فيه ويصدق في قوله أردت ما يحدث ملكي فيه في المستقبل فيعتق عليه بإقراره كما إذا قال زينب طالق وله امرأة معروفة بهذا الاسم ثم قال لي امرأة أخرى بهذا الاسم عنيتها طلقت المعروفة بظاهر اللفظ والمجهولة باعترافه كذا ها هنا. وكذا لو قال كل مملوك أملكه الساعة فهو حر إن هذا يقع على ما في ملكه وقت اليمين ولا يعتق ما يستفيده بعد ذلك إلا أن يكون نوى ذلك فيلزمه ما نوى لأن المراد من الساعة المذكورة هي الساعة المعروفة عند الناس وهي الحال لا الساعة الزمانية التي يذكرها المنجمون فيتناول هذا الكلام من كان في ملكه وقت التكلم لا من يستفيده من بعد، فإن قال أردت به من أستفيده في هذه الساعة الزمانية يصدق فيه لأن اللفظ يحتمله وفيه تشديد على نفسه ولكن لا يصدق في صرف اللفظ عمن يكون في ملكه للحال، وسواء أطلق أو علق بشرط قدم الشرط أو أخره؛ كذا في البدائع .
And if he presents the condition and says, 'If I speak to so-and-so, or when I speak to so-and-so, or when tomorrow comes, then every slave I purchase is free,' then this applies to what he purchases after speaking, not before. Even if he had purchased slaves before speaking and then speaks, not one of them is freed, and what he purchases afterwards is freed. And if he says, 'Every slave I purchase when I enter the house is free,' or says 'If so-and-so arrives,' then this applies to what he purchases after the action he swore upon, and what he purchased before that is not freed unless he specifies them. (Regarding his statement: 'and if he did not say 'that day'?') He said: No, meaning he does not free what he owns after it, rather he only frees from the time of speaking because his statement 'every slave of mine' is specific to the present, and the consequence is the freedom of the slave in the present, attached to the slave in the present, i.e., the freedom of the slave in the present is the consequence. It is for the present because the chosen view for the description from the active and passive participle is that its meaning is established at the time of speaking to the one to whom it is attributed, in the manner of its establishment or occurrence, and the 'lam' is for specification. So if he had nothing in his possession on the day he swore, the oath was void. There is no difference between whether the emancipation is conditional as in the book or immediate, or whether the condition is presented first or later, and whether the conditional is 'in' as in the book or other words like 'idha dakhalti' or 'idha ma' or 'mata' or 'mata ma'. And his statement 'for me' is not a restriction because if he said 'every slave I own is free' and there is no intention, then it is only for the oath because the form 'if'al' (present verb) is used for present and future, but when unrestricted, it is intended for the present by custom, law, and language. As for custom, if someone says 'so-and-so eats or drinks or does such-and-such', he intends the present. A man says 'I do not own a thousand dirhams' meaning the present. As for law, if someone says 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah', he becomes a believer; if he says 'I bear witness that so-and-so has a right against so-and-so', he is a witness. As for language, this form is established for the present as original because there is no other form for the present, and for the future there are 'sa' and 'sawfa', so the present is original and the future is peripheral. Therefore, when unrestricted, it defaults to the present. If he says 'I intended what I will own in the future', he frees what he owns at present and the future acquisitions? Because we mentioned that its apparent meaning is for the present and his intention turns it from its apparent meaning, so he is not believed in that, but he is believed in his statement 'I intended what I will own in the future' and he frees by his confession, just as if he said 'Zainab is divorced' and he has a wife known by this name, then he says 'I intended another woman by this name', the known one is divorced by the apparent wording, and the unknown one by his admission, similarly here. And likewise, if he said 'every slave I own now is free', this applies to what is in his possession at the time of the oath, and what he acquires afterwards is not freed unless he intended that, then it binds him for what he intended because the intended meaning of 'now' is the time known to people, which is the present, not the temporal hour mentioned by astronomers. This speech includes those who were in his possession at the time of speaking, not those he acquires later. If he says 'I intended those I acquire in this temporal hour', he is believed in it because the wording can carry it and there is hardship on himself, but he is not believed in turning the wording away from those in his possession at present. And whether it is absolute or conditional, with the condition presented first or later, as in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding oaths by emancipation. The author begins by presenting a ruling: when a person makes a conditional oath of emancipation, and the condition is phrased as 'if I speak...' or 'when I speak...' or 'when tomorrow comes, every slave I purchase is free,' the emancipation applies only to slaves purchased after the condition is fulfilled (e.g., after speaking), not to those owned before. This is because the phrase 'every slave I purchase' refers to future acquisitions only. If he says 'every slave I purchase when I enter the house,' again, it only includes slaves purchased after entering. The exception is if he explicitly intends to include past acquisitions, but the default is future. The author then explains the general principle: when someone says 'every slave of mine is free' (or 'every slave I own') without specifying a time, it refers to slaves currently in his possession at the time of the oath. This is based on Arabic usage: the word 'mamluk' (owned) is a passive participle (ism maf'ul) and indicates something existing at the time of speaking. The letter 'li' (my) indicates possession at the present moment. Therefore, if he owns no slaves when he swears, the oath is void (laghw). The ruling applies whether the emancipation is conditional or immediate, whether the condition is presented first or later, and whether the conditional particle is 'in', 'idha', or others. The author then discusses the phrase 'kullu mamluk amlikuhu' (every slave I acquire) and argues that the verb 'amlikuhu' (I acquire) is in the present tense form (fi'l mudari') which, when used without a future indicator, signifies the present moment by default, based on 'urf (custom), shar' (legal usage), and lugha (language). If the speaker intends future acquisition, he is not believed in court (qada'an) because the apparent meaning is present, but he is believed in his conscience (diyanatan) because the language can bear that meaning, similar to the case of pronouncing divorce on a wife. The passage also discusses the word 'al-sa'ah' (the hour) and its common meaning as 'the present time', not the astronomical hour. Thus, if someone says 'every slave I own this hour is free,' it applies only to those in his possession at the moment of the oath, unless he intended a different meaning. The author concludes that the ruling applies whether the oath is absolute or conditional, and whether the condition is presented first or later, as stated in al-Bada'i'.

Key Terms
اشتريه (ashtarihi): I purchase it (present tense verb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'If I enter the house, every slave I purchase is free.' He enters the house. He had purchased a slave before entering and another after entering. Under the Hanafi ruling, which slaves are freed?
A) Both slaves are freed because the phrase 'every slave I purchase' is general.
B) Only the slave purchased after entering is freed.
C) Only the slave purchased before entering is freed.
D) Neither slave is freed because the condition is not related to purchase.

Key Takeaway
In conditional emancipation oaths, the phrase 'every slave I purchase' only frees slaves bought after the condition is met, not those owned beforehand.

12 min
Default of Present Ownership in 'Every Slave I Own'
Linguistic analysis of 'kullu mamluk li' defaulting to present ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school interprets 'every slave I own' as referring only to slaves currently owned at the time of the oath, and identify the linguistic, customary, and legal evidence supporting this interpretation.

Core Idea
'Every slave I own' defaults to present ownership because the passive participle and the present tense verb indicate the time of speaking.

When someone swears, 'Every slave I own is free,' does it apply to future acquisitions? The Hanafi school, through a detailed linguistic analysis of the terms 'mamluk' (owned) and 'amlikuhu' (I own), establishes that the default meaning is present ownership. This lesson unpacks the evidence from language, custom, and shari'ah, and shows how this ruling is consistently applied even in cases involving the word 'al-sa'ah' (the hour).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that the phrase 'every slave of mine' (kullu mamluk li) is specific to the present moment. This is based on the analysis of the passive participle 'mamluk' and the possessive 'li', which together indicate current possession.
If he owns no slaves at the time of the oath, the oath is void (laghw).
2
Analyze the verbal form 'amlikuhu'
The verb 'amlikuhu' (I own/I possess) is in the mudari' form. The author argues that, when unrestricted, this form defaults to the present tense in 'urf (custom), shar' (legal usage), and lugha (language). Evidence is given from everyday speech, shahada, and grammatical structure.
The future tense has distinct markers like 'sa' and 'sawfa', so the mudari' alone is primarily present.
3
Apply the analogy with divorce
The author compares with a man who says 'Zainab is divorced' and then claims he intended another woman. The court holds him to the apparent meaning (the known wife), but he is believed in conscience if he intended a different woman. Similarly, if he intends future ownership, he is not believed in court but is believed diyanatan.
This analogy reinforces that the apparent meaning binds in qada, but intention is considered in diyanah.
4
Consider the case of 'al-sa'ah'
If he says 'every slave I own this hour (al-sa'ah) is free', the default meaning of 'al-sa'ah' is the present time, not the astronomical hour. It still refers to current ownership, unless he intends a specific astronomical hour, which he may be believed in conscience.
The term 'al-sa'ah' in common usage means 'now', not a sixty-minute period.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is that 'every slave I own/possess' in an oath of emancipation applies only to slaves in his possession at the time of the oath. Any intention to include future acquisitions is ineffective in court but considered in conscience.
This applies whether the oath is absolute or conditional, and regardless of the order of condition.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding oaths by emancipation. The author begins by presenting a ruling: when a person makes a conditional oath of emancipation, and the condition is phrased as 'if I speak...' or 'when I speak...' or 'when tomorrow comes, every slave I purchase is free,' the emancipation applies only to slaves purchased after the condition is fulfilled (e.g., after speaking), not to those owned before. This is because the phrase 'every slave I purchase' refers to future acquisitions only. If he says 'every slave I purchase when I enter the house,' again, it only includes slaves purchased after entering. The exception is if he explicitly intends to include past acquisitions, but the default is future. The author then explains the general principle: when someone says 'every slave of mine is free' (or 'every slave I own') without specifying a time, it refers to slaves currently in his possession at the time of the oath. This is based on Arabic usage: the word 'mamluk' (owned) is a passive participle (ism maf'ul) and indicates something existing at the time of speaking. The letter 'li' (my) indicates possession at the present moment. Therefore, if he owns no slaves when he swears, the oath is void (laghw). The ruling applies whether the emancipation is conditional or immediate, whether the condition is presented first or later, and whether the conditional particle is 'in', 'idha', or others. The author then discusses the phrase 'kullu mamluk amlikuhu' (every slave I acquire) and argues that the verb 'amlikuhu' (I acquire) is in the present tense form (fi'l mudari') which, when used without a future indicator, signifies the present moment by default, based on 'urf (custom), shar' (legal usage), and lugha (language). If the speaker intends future acquisition, he is not believed in court (qada'an) because the apparent meaning is present, but he is believed in his conscience (diyanatan) because the language can bear that meaning, similar to the case of pronouncing divorce on a wife. The passage also discusses the word 'al-sa'ah' (the hour) and its common meaning as 'the present time', not the astronomical hour. Thus, if someone says 'every slave I own this hour is free,' it applies only to those in his possession at the moment of the oath, unless he intended a different meaning. The author concludes that the ruling applies whether the oath is absolute or conditional, and whether the condition is presented first or later, as stated in al-Bada'i'.

Key Terms
كل مملوك لي (kullu mamluk li): Every slave that is in my possession at the time of the oath
صيغة افعل (sighatu if'al): The present tense verb form (e.g., yamliku) which is used for present and future but defaults to present when unqualified.
الساعة (al-sa'ah): The hour; in this context, the present moment or time of speaking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'Every slave I own is free.' At the time, he has three slaves. Later, he inherits a fourth. Under the Hanafi ruling, which slaves are freed?
A) All four slaves are freed because 'own' can mean present and future.
B) Only the three slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed.
C) Only the inherited slave is freed because it is a new acquisition.
D) None are freed because the oath is invalid if he later acquires more.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'every slave I own' in an emancipation oath defaults to present ownership, supported by linguistic, legal, and customary proofs.

Exclusion of the Fetus from 'Mamluk' in Oaths
Ruling that 'mamluk' does not include the fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school excludes the fetus from the term 'mamluk' in emancipation oaths and apply this rule to the four illustrative scenarios given in the text.

Core Idea
The fetus is not an independent slave; it is freed only as a subsidiary of the mother.

When a person makes an oath involving the freeing of slaves, the exact meaning of the term 'mamluk' becomes crucial. Does it include a fetus in the womb? The Hanafi school, through linguistic analysis and examples, clearly states it does not. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that exclusion and its practical application in several case studies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State clearly that the term 'mamluk' does not include the fetus (haml). The fetus is not considered an independent slave for purposes of oaths or other transactions.
2
Examine the Proofs
The passage gives two main proofs: (1) the fetus is a slave only as a subsidiary of the mother, not independently; (2) the fetus is considered a limb in some respects, but 'mamluk' applies to whole persons, not limbs.
Also note that the fetus cannot be sold separately and its emancipation does not fulfill expiation.
3
Analyze the Three Scenarios
Work through the three examples where the fetus is not freed: (a) bequest for 'every slave and his fetus' – only fetus inherits; (b) freeing 'every male slave' – pregnant female gives birth; (c) buying two slaves – buys a pregnant female. In each, explain why the fetus is excluded.
The fourth scenario (saying 'every slave of mine is free' without restriction) includes the mother and the fetus as subsidiary.
4
Understand the Linguistic Principle
The term 'mamluk' (masculine form) is more general than 'mamlukah' (feminine). It includes females unless specified otherwise. This is why a general oath like 'every slave of mine is free' includes pregnant female slaves and the fetus follows.
The author cites Fath al-Qadir: usage has established generality.
5
Distinguish Intention (Niyyah) Scenarios
When someone says 'every slave of mine' but intends only males, he is believed diyanatan but not qada'an. However, when he says 'my slaves, all of them are free' (mamaliki kulluhum), the emphasis 'kulluhum' blocks specification even diyanatan. Understand the difference based on the presence or absence of emphasis.
This shows the importance of wording in legal oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمملوك لا يتناول الحمل لأن اللفظ يتناول والمملوك المطلق والجنين مملوك تبعاً للأم لا مقصوداً، ولأنه عضو من وجه واسم المملوك يتناول الأنفس دون الأعضاء ولهذا لا يسمى يملك بيعه منفرداً ولا يجزئ عتقه عن الكفارة، فلو قال كل مملوك لي وله حمل أوصى له به دون أمه، أو قال كل مملوك لي ذكر فهو حر وله جارية حامل فولدت ذكراً لأقل من ستة أشهر، أو قال إن اشتريت مملوكين فهما حران فاشترى جارية حاملاً فإن الحمل في هذه الصور الثلاث لا يعتق لما ذكرنا، ولا تعتق الأم في المسألة الثانية أيضاً لتقييده بالذكورة، ولا مسألة الثالثة كما في البدائع لأن شرط الحنث شراء مملوكين والحمل لا مملوكاً على الإطلاق، وكذا لو قال للحامل كل مملوك لي غيرك حر لم يعتق الحمل كما في المحيط. وإنما قيدنا بالصور الأربع لأنه لو قال كل مملوك لي حر وله جارية حاملة فإن الحامل تدخل فيعتق الحمل تبعاً لها كما في الهداية وهذا بناء على أن لفظة مملوك إما لذات متصفة بالمملوكية وقيد التذكير ليس جزء المفهوم، وإذا كان التأنيث جزء مفهوم مملوكة فيكون مملوك أعم من مملوكة فالثابت فيه عدم الدلالة على التأنيث لا الدلالة على عدم التأنيث. وأما أن الاستعمال استمر فيه على الأعمية فوجب اعتباره كذلك ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. قيد بعدم تناول الحمل فقط لأنه يتناول العبيد ولو مرهونين أو مأذونين أو مأجورين والإماء وإن كن حوامل وأمهات أولاده وأولادهما والمدبر والمدبرة ولو نوى الذكور فقط لم يصدق في القضاء لأنه خلاف الظاهر في عرف الاستعمال ويصدق ديانة مع أن طائفة من الأصوليين على أن جمع النساء حقيقة وضعاً. وفي الذخيرة : قال مماليكي كلهم أحرار ونوى الرجال دون النساء لم يذكره وقالوا : لا يصدق ديانة بخلاف قوله كل مملوك لي ونوى التخصيص يصدق ديانة ا هـ . فإن قلت : ما الفرق وفي الوجهين نية تخصيص العام؟ فالجواب أن كلهم تأكيد للعام قبله وهو مماليكي لأنه جمع مضاف فيعم وهو يرفع احتمال المجاز غالباً والتخصيص يوجب المجاز فلا يجوز بخلاف قوله كل مملوك لي فإن الثابت به أصل العموم فقط فقبل التخصيص. وفي المحيط : لو قال لم أنو المدبرين قيل لم يدين قضاء وديانة، والصحيح أنه يصدق ديانة لأنه لا يمكن تخصيص العام إلا باعتبار الوصف فإن الخصوص لا يمتاز عن العام إلا باعتبار الوصف فلو لم يصح التخصيص في حق الوصف ما أمكن تخصيص عام أبداً ا هـ.
And 'mamluk' does not include the fetus because the word refers to an absolute slave, and the fetus is a slave only as a subsidiary of the mother, not independently; and because it is a limb in one respect, and the name 'mamluk' applies to persons, not limbs. For this reason, it cannot be sold separately, and its emancipation is not valid for expiation. Thus, if he says: 'Every slave of mine and his fetus' — he makes a bequest for the fetus without the mother; or he says: 'Every male slave of mine is free' and he has a pregnant female slave who gives birth to a male before six months; or he says: 'If I purchase two slaves, they are free' and he purchases a pregnant female slave — in these three cases, the fetus is not freed for the reasons mentioned. The mother is not freed in the second case due to the restriction to males, nor in the third case, as in al-Bada'i', because the condition for breach is purchasing two slaves, and the fetus is not an absolute slave. Similarly, if he says to a pregnant woman: 'Every slave of mine except you is free,' the fetus is not freed, as in al-Muhit. We only restricted ourselves to four cases because if he says: 'Every slave of mine is free' and he has a pregnant female slave, then the pregnant woman is included, and the fetus is freed as her subsidiary, as in al-Hidayah, and this is based on that the word 'mamluk' is for a being characterized by being owned, and the restriction of masculinity is not part of the concept. When femininity is part of the concept of 'mamlukah', then 'mamluk' is more general than 'mamlukah', so what is established is the lack of indication of femininity, not indication of lack of femininity. Since usage has continued on this generality, it must be considered so; as in Fath al-Qadir. He restricted to not including the fetus only because it includes slaves even if mortgaged, or authorized, or hired, and female slaves even if pregnant, and mothers of children and their children, and mudabbar and mudabbarah. If he intended only males, he is not believed in court because it contradicts the apparent in customary usage, but he is believed diyanatan, even though a group of usul scholars hold that the plural of women is a real (haqiqah) form. In al-Dhakhirah: He said 'My slaves, all of them are free' and intended males only, not females; it was not mentioned, and they said: He is not believed diyanatan, unlike his statement 'every slave of mine' and he intended specification, he is believed diyanatan. End quote. If you say: What is the difference when in both cases there is an intention to specify the general? The answer: That 'all of them' is an emphasis of the general before it, which is 'my slaves' because it is a genitive construct (idafah) and is general, and it removes the possibility of majaz usually, and specification necessitates majaz, so it is not allowed, unlike his statement 'every slave of mine' because what is established is only the principle of generality, so specification is accepted. And in al-Muhit: If he said 'I did not intend the mudabbar slaves', it was said that he is not believed in court or diyanatan; the correct view is that he is believed diyanatan because it is impossible to specify a general except by considering the description, for what is specific cannot be distinguished from the general except by considering the description. So if specification were not valid with regard to description, it would never be possible to specify a general at all. End quote.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage establishes that the term 'mamluk' (slave) does not include the fetus (haml) in the womb. The author gives several proofs: (1) the fetus is a slave only as an accessory to the mother, not independently; (2) the fetus is considered a limb in some respects, and the word 'mamluk' applies to persons, not limbs; (3) the fetus cannot be sold separately, and its emancipation is not sufficient for expiation. Three examples clarify: (a) if someone makes a bequest for 'every slave of mine and his fetus,' only the fetus inherits, not the mother; (b) if someone says 'every male slave of mine is free' and his pregnant slave gives birth to a male before six months, the mother is not freed (due to the restriction), but the fetus is not freed either because it was not a 'mamluk' at the time of the oath; (c) if someone vows to free two slaves he purchases, and he buys a pregnant slave, the fetus is not counted as a slave, so the condition is not met. However, if he simply says 'every slave of mine is free' and has a pregnant slave, the mother is included and the fetus is freed as a subsidiary, following the principle that 'mamluk' includes females (since it is a generic masculine). The author then explains that the term 'mamluk' includes various categories: mortgaged, authorized, hired slaves, umm walad, mudabbar, etc. A discussion follows about intention (niyyah) to specify: if someone says 'every slave of mine is free' but intends only males, he is not believed in court but is believed diyanatan. The difference between 'kullu mamluk li' and 'mamaliki kulluhum ahraar' is explained: in the latter, the word 'kulluhum' emphasizes generality and removes the possibility of majaz, so specification is not accepted even diyanatan. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Muhit about not intending mudabbar slaves.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): The fetus in the womb, not considered an independent slave.
مملوك على الإطلاق (mamluk 'ala al-itlaq): An absolute slave, an independent human being recognized as property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'If I buy two slaves, they are free.' He then buys a pregnant female slave. According to the Hanafi school, is his oath broken?
A) Yes, because the female slave counts as one and the fetus as another, totaling two.
B) No, because the fetus is not considered a 'mamluk' and thus only one slave was purchased.
C) Yes, only if the fetus is male.
D) No, because oaths are not taken seriously in this context.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the term 'mamluk' excludes the fetus unless the mother is freed, because the fetus is a subsidiary and not an independent person.

Scope of 'Mamluk': Excluded Categories and Dispute on Trader Slave's Slaves
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of 'mamluk' in emancipation oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which categories of slaves are excluded from the term 'mamluk' in an oath of emancipation and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the slaves of a trader slave.

Core Idea
Not all slaves under your ownership are your 'mamalik' — mukatab, mushtarak, and sub-slaves require special analysis.

In oaths of emancipation, the term 'mamluk' does not automatically cover every slave in one's possession. This lesson examines three categories that are excluded or conditionally included: the mukatab (a slave under a manumission contract), the mushtarak (a co-owned slave), and the slaves of a trader slave (a slave who owns other slaves). The last case generates a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying a master's general oath to free 'all his slaves.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the excluded categories
The passage begins by stating that the term 'mamluk' does not include anything that is not a slave in the absolute sense. It then specifies three categories: the mukatab, the mushtarak (co-owned slave), and the slaves of a trader slave (عبيد عبده التاجر). List these three as distinct cases.
The passage also mentions the fetus earlier, but that is outside the scope of this lesson.
2
Understand the rationale for each exclusion
For the mukatab: because he is 'free in hand' (حر يداً), his ownership status is partial, so he is not considered a full mamluk. For the mushtarak: such a slave is owned by multiple partners and is only included if the speaker specifically intends them. For the trader slave's slaves: the ruling is disputed among the Hanafi imams.
The mukatab also does not fall under the word 'abd, as established earlier in the text.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the trader slave's slaves
Abu Yusuf holds that the sub-slaves are never included, regardless of the trader slave's debt. Muhammad holds that they are always freed, whether intended or not, and regardless of debt. Abu Hanifa conditions the ruling on the presence of debt: if the trader slave has debt, the sub-slaves are not freed; if no debt, they are freed. The passage cuts off before completing Abu Hanifa's view, but the full ruling is understood from other sources.
This is a case of 'intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf' (dispute within the school).
4
Apply the dispute to a real scenario
Consider a master who says, 'Every slave of mine is free.' He owns a slave (Zayd) who is a trader and himself owns two slaves (Bakr and Umar). According to Abu Yusuf, Bakr and Umar are not freed. According to Muhammad, they are freed. According to Abu Hanifa, they are freed only if Zayd has no debt.
The mufta bihi (operative position) for fatwa is generally Abu Yusuf's view, as it is the most practical and avoids complications.
5
Note the mufta bihi position
Later Hanafi authorities, including the author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, often prefer Abu Yusuf's view that the sub-slaves are not included in the master's general oath. This is the safest position in practice.
The text does not explicitly give a tarjih, but the placement of Abu Yusuf's view first may indicate preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بعدم تناوله للحمل إلى أنه لا يتناول ما لم يكن مملوكاً على الإطلاق فلا يتناول المكاتب لأنه مملوك من وجه إذ هو حر يداً، وقدمنا أنه لا يدخل تحت لفظ العبد أيضاً ولا يتناول المشترك إلا بالنية ولا عبيد عبده التاجر وهو قول أبي يوسف، سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا، وعلى قول محمد عتقوا نواهم أو لا عليه دين أو لا، وعلى قول أبي حنيفة إن لم
And he indicated by not including the fetus that [the term] does not include what is not a slave absolutely. So it does not include a mukatab because he is a slave in one respect, being free in terms of hand. We have already presented that he also does not fall under the word 'abd. And it does not include a shared [slave] except with intention. And it does not include the slaves of his slave-trader slave; this is the view of Abu Yusuf, whether or not the slave has debt. According to Muhammad, they are freed whether he intended them or not, whether there is debt or not. According to Abu Hanifa, if not...
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of what the term 'mamluk' in an oath of emancipation does not include. The author states that the exclusion of the fetus implies that the term does not cover anything that is not a 'mamluk' in the absolute sense. Therefore, it does not include a mukatab (a slave who has made a contract of manumission) because he is free in terms of his hand (i.e., he has the right to earn and pay, and his ownership is incomplete). The author mentions that the mukatab also does not fall under the word 'abd, as established earlier. Similarly, a mushtarak (a slave owned by multiple partners) is not included unless the speaker specifically intends them. Then the text addresses a specific case: the slaves of a slave trader (عبيد عبده التاجر). This refers to a scenario where a person owns a slave who himself is a trader and owns other slaves. The question is: if the master makes a general oath to free all his slaves, are the slaves of his trader slave included? The Hanafi school has a dispute. According to Abu Yusuf, they are not included, regardless of whether the trader slave owes debt or not. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, they are included and become free whether he intended them or not, and regardless of debt. According to Abu Hanifa, the ruling depends on whether the trader slave has debt or not; if he has debt, the sub-slaves are not freed because the slave's ownership is considered incomplete. The text cuts off at 'إن لم' (if not), but the full ruling is that if there is no debt, they are freed according to Abu Hanifa. This is a classic intra-madhhab dispute recorded in the zahir al-riwayah and commented upon in later works.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) and is considered free in hand (حر يداً) but still a slave in terms of ownership.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A slave owned by multiple partners (shuyu'), considered a 'mamluk' but only partially owned by each partner.
عبيد عبده التاجر (ubaydu 'abdihi al-tajir): The slaves owned by a slave who himself is a trader (i.e., a slave who owns other slaves).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a master says 'Every slave of mine is free' and he has a slave who is a trader (tajir) and who himself owns slaves, are those sub-slaves freed?
A) Yes, they are freed because they are under the master's ownership indirectly.
B) No, they are not freed because the trader slave's ownership is incomplete.
C) Yes, but only if the trader slave has no debt.
D) No, unless the master intended them.

Key Takeaway
The term 'mamluk' in emancipation oaths does not automatically cover every slave; the mukatab, mushtarak (without intention), and—according to the mufta bihi—the slaves of a trader slave are excluded.

Scope of Manumission Oath: 'Own Tomorrow' Dispute
Explaining scope of manumission oath with future time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding slaves included in an oath of manumission with a future time phrase and explain the linguistic basis for the difference.

Core Idea
When 'tomorrow' modifies ownership, Muhammad includes both current and future slaves; Abu Yusuf limits to future only.

This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqā'iq delves into a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does an oath like 'every slave I own tomorrow is free' also free slaves already owned today? The answer turns on whether the future adverb 'tomorrow' qualifies the act of ownership or the resulting freedom. Two great Imams, Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf, offer different rulings, each grounded in a precise reading of the language. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to parse temporal phrases in legal oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrasing
Examine the two formulations: 'كل مملوك أملكه غداً' (every slave I own tomorrow) and 'كل مملوك أملكه إذا جاء غد' (every slave I own when tomorrow comes). Note how the adverb is attached.
The same Arabic verb 'أملكه' is used but the time element shifts.
2
State the two positions
Imam Muhammad (in al-Jami') holds that both slaves owned now and those acquired tomorrow are freed. Abu Yusuf restricts to only those acquired tomorrow.
This is recorded in the narration of Ibn Samā'ah from Muhammad.
3
Analyze the linguistic cause
Muhammad sees 'tomorrow' as potentially qualifying the freedom, so the ownership verb remains generic enough to include the present. Abu Yusuf reads 'I own tomorrow' as explicitly future ownership, excluding the present.
The grammatical analysis of the present tense (المضارع) is crucial: it can indicate present or future depending on context.
4
Apply to other time phrases
The same dispute applies to 'رأس شهر كذا' (beginning of month) and 'يوم الجمعة' (Friday). Custom (urf) helps define the exact time, but the principle remains.
The passage gives examples from Abu Yusuf to illustrate his consistent approach.
5
Distinguish conditional phrasing
When the phrase is conditional ('إذا جاء غد'), both Imams agree it refers to currently owned slaves, with freedom delayed to the fulfilment of the condition.
This unanimity shows the dispute is purely about the temporal usage, not the conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كل مملوك لي أو أملكه فهو حر بعد غد أو بعد موتي يتناول من ملكه منذ حلف فقط) لما قدمنا أن قوله كل مملوك لي للحال وكذا كل مملوك أملكه لأن المضارع للحال كما بيناه ، فمن كان في ملكه وقت اليمين يصير حراً في المسألتين بعد غد. وفي قوله بعد موتي يصير من في ملكه وقت اليمين مدبراً في المسألتين فلا يعتق من اشتراه بعد اليمين في التقييد بقوله بعد موتي . قيد بكون الظرف ظرفاً للحرية لأنه لو جعله ظرفاً للملك كما إذا قال كل مملوك أملكه غداً فهو حر ولا نيه له ذكر محمد في الجامع أنه يعتق كل من ملکه في غد ومن كان في ملكه قبله . وقال أبو يوسف . لا يعتق إلا من استفاد ملكه في غد ولا يعتق من جاء غد وهو في وهو رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد. وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قال كل مملوك أملكه رأس شهر كذا فهو حر ورأس الشهر الليلة التي يهل فيها الهلال ومن الغد إلى الليل للعرف. وعن أبي يوسف فيمن قال كل مملوك أملكه يوم الجمعة فهو حر قال : ليس هذا على ما في ملكه إنما هو على ما يملكه يوم الجمعة، وهذا على أصل أبي يوسف صحيح لأنه أضاف العتق إلى زمان مستقبل، فأما إذا قال كل مملوك أملكه إذا جاء غد فهو حر فهذا على ما في ملكه في قولهم لأنه جعل مجيء الغد شرطاً لثبوت العتق لا غير فيعتق من في ملكه ولكن عند مجيء الغد؛ كذا في البدائع .
His statement: (Every slave of mine or that I own is free after tomorrow or after my death includes only those he owned since the oath) because we established that his statement 'every slave of mine' is for the present, and likewise 'every slave I own' because the present tense indicates the present as we explained. So whoever was in his ownership at the time of the oath becomes free in both issues after tomorrow. And in his statement 'after my death', whoever was in his ownership at the time of the oath becomes a mudabbar in both issues, so whoever he buys after the oath is not freed due to the restriction 'after my death'. He restricted the adverb being an adverb for freedom, because if he made it an adverb for ownership, as if he said 'every slave I own tomorrow is free' with no intention, Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' that it frees everyone he owned tomorrow and whoever was in his ownership before it. Abu Yusuf said: It frees only the one he acquires ownership of tomorrow, and it does not free the one who comes tomorrow while he is... And this is a narration of Ibn Samā'ah from Muhammad. And on this difference, if he said 'every slave I own at the beginning of such a month is free', and the beginning of the month is the night the crescent appears, and from the next day until night according to custom. And from Abu Yusuf regarding someone who said 'every slave I own on Friday is free', he said: This is not about what he owns, but about what he will own on Friday. And this is correct according to Abu Yusuf's principle because he attached freedom to a future time. But if he said 'every slave I own when tomorrow comes is free', then this is about what he owns according to their statement because he made the coming of tomorrow a condition for the affirmation of freedom, not else, so he frees whoever he owns, but at the coming of tomorrow; thus in al-Badā'i'.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqā'iq explains the ruling on an oath of manumission phrased with a future time or condition. The commentator expands on the matn's statement. The core issue is whether the phrase 'every slave I own' refers to slaves currently owned or those acquired later. When the master says 'after tomorrow' or 'after my death', the phrase applies only to slaves owned at the time of the oath, because the present tense indicates the present. However, if the master says 'I own tomorrow' making 'tomorrow' an adverb for ownership, then Muhammad holds that it includes both those owned tomorrow and those owned before. Abu Yusuf restricts it to only those acquired tomorrow. The difference hinges on whether the future time is attached to freedom or to ownership. When the phrase is conditional ('if tomorrow comes'), it applies to slaves owned now, with freedom occurring at the condition's fulfilment. This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of temporal and conditional phrases.

Key Terms
مُدَبَّر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death, becoming a tadbir contract.
ظَرْف (zarf): An adverb of time or place; here specifically temporal adverb like 'tomorrow' or 'after my death'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the master says 'كل مملوك أملكه غداً فهو حر', what is the difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf?
A) Muhammad says only future slaves; Abu Yusuf includes present ones.
B) Muhammad includes present and future; Abu Yusuf only future.
C) Both agree it includes only present slaves.
D) Both agree it includes only future slaves.

Key Takeaway
The attachment of the future adverb to freedom versus ownership determines whether currently owned slaves are included in the manumission oath.

Manumission for a Payment (al-Itq bi Ja'l)
Introducing the ruling on a master freeing his slave for a payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling and conditions of manumission for a payment (ja'l) and identify the status of the slave before payment.

Core Idea
A slave can be freed for a specified payment; acceptance makes it binding, but freedom is only upon payment.

This lesson introduces a unique Hanafi contract where a master offers freedom to his slave in exchange for a payment. The slave's acceptance creates a binding obligation, but the actual manumission occurs only after the payment is made. Before payment, the slave's status changes to a 'ma'dhūn' (permitted to trade), which has practical implications for his ability to earn and contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that it is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) for a master to free his slave for a payment (ja'l) if the slave accepts. The slave becomes free upon acceptance, but the payment is wajib (obligatory).
This is a binding contract, similar to a sale of freedom.
2
Distinguish unconditional vs. conditional freedom
If freedom is conditioned upon payment (e.g., 'you are free if you pay me X'), the slave does not become free immediately. Instead, he becomes a ma'dhūn (permitted to trade) until he pays. Upon payment (takhliyah), he is free.
The term 'takhliyah' here means paying the amount, literally 'vacating' the obligation.
3
Note the definition of ja'l
Ja'l (with dammah on jīm) linguistically means a payment given to a worker for his work. Here, it is the consideration for manumission.
This clarifies that the payment is in exchange for the act of freeing, not a sale of the slave's person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العتق على جعل. يصح أن يحرر عبده على مال فقبل عتق ولو علق عتقه بأدائه صار مأذوناً وعتق بالتخلية. والجعل في اللغة بضم الجيم ما يجعل للعامل على عمله ثم قوله : (ولا يؤدي منه عنه ويعتق كذا في الفتح والظاهر أنه يقرأ ويعتق بالنصب بأن مضمرة.

And the term 'ja'l' in language (with dammah on the jīm) is what is given to a worker for his work. Then his statement: (And he does not pay from it and his being free thus in al-Fatḥ. And it appears that it is read with nasb 'yu'taqa' with an implied 'an'.

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on manumission for a consideration (kitāb al-‘itq bi ja‘l). It states a clear ruling: if a master says to his slave 'you are free if you pay me such amount', and the slave accepts, he becomes free and must pay. If the freedom is conditioned upon payment, the slave becomes a ma'dhūn (slave permitted to trade) until he pays, and upon payment (takhliyah—literally 'vacating', i.e., paying the amount), he is free. The lexical note clarifies that 'ja'l' refers to a payment for work. The final sentence is a textual note from the commentator about a reading variant in al-Fatḥ: 'wa ya'tiq' should be read with nasb (subjunctive) after 'an (that), meaning 'it is apparent that he is freed'.

Key Terms
جَعْل (ja‘l): In this context, payment given by the slave to the master in exchange for freedom.
مَأْذُون (ma’dhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; here, the slave is permitted until he pays the ja'l.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The contract is invalid because it is conditional

Key Takeaway
Manumission for a payment is valid in Hanafi fiqh; if conditioned on payment, the slave becomes a ma'dhūn and gains freedom only upon payment.

Combining Immediate and Deferred Manumission: Tadbir and Wasiyyah in One Statement
Analyzing whether conditional manumission for payment constitutes tadbir or wasiyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates combining immediate emancipation and a future bequest in one statement, and evaluate the refinement offered by Fath al-Qadir using taqdir.

Core Idea
A single statement can effect both immediate tadbir and deferred wasiyyah through different legal aspects, not contradiction.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in slave emancipation: when a master says 'You are free for a payment' with a condition, the statement produces both an immediate tadbir and a future wasiyyah. Al-Hidayah treats this as valid through two considerations, while Fath al-Qadir refines it by estimating an implicit wording at death. Understanding this internal Hanafi debate sharpens your skill in applying usul al-fiqh tools like taqdir and the principle of dual aspects (i'tibaran).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Determine what the master’s statement produces: if unconditional, immediate freedom upon acceptance; if conditioned on payment, the slave becomes a mudabbar (deferred emancipation) and authorized to earn the payment.
2
Locate the two aspects in al-Hidayah
Observe that al-Hidayah analyzes the statement from two perspectives: immediate emancipation (tadbir) for the present slave, and a bequest (wasiyyah) for future slaves owned at death.
3
Understand the objection from the 'Iraqi' position
The Iraqis argue that combining present and future in one wording is impossible; a single speech act cannot span contradictory temporal references.
4
Apply the refinement from Fath al-Qadir: taqdir
Fath al-Qadir resolves the objection by estimating an implicit separate wording at death ('every slave I own is free'), so the future effect is not from the original statement but from an implied one, avoiding any conflict.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: both the current slave and future acquisitions are freed subject to the one-third rule of bequests and debt payment, as per the ruling of mudabbar after the master’s death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He freed his slave for a payment, and he [the slave] accepted, then he becomes free. If he conditioned his freedom on payment, he becomes authorized and becomes free by relinquishing [the debt?]. And from the aspect that it is a bequest, it includes what he purchases, considering the waiting state, which is the state of death, and before death the state of ownership is pure future, so it does not fall under the wording. At the time of death, it becomes as if he said: 'Every slave I own is free', unlike if he said 'after tomorrow' as previously, because it is a single action, which is the obligation of emancipation, and there is no bequest in it, and the state is pure future, so they differ. The cause for the estimation is what we mentioned of realizing the purpose of the bequest of reward and piety for the relatives. This cause does not require the estimation to be made at the time of owning the slave, otherwise he would be an absolute mudabbar; rather it is needed at his death from his saying... so do not hold on to it. His statement at the time of ownership is not explicit because it only covers the present, nor is it estimated because its estimation is delayed to before death, so he is neither an absolute mudabbar nor a restricted one. This resolves the issue. End. The summary is that the emancipation of what he later owns at his death is not from the mentioned wording, so as to necessitate the combination of present and future; rather it is from another estimated wording, indicated by the realization of his purpose for reward. So there is no combination in one wording, but rather two wordings: mentioned and estimated. And he benefited from his saying 'from his third' that if both come out of the third, both are completely freed. If it is insufficient for both, each is prorated by his value. If the master has a debt that encompasses, then both serve for all their values, as is the ruling of the mudabbar after his master's death. The author indicated that if he said: 'Every slave I own is free when I die', the ruling is likewise. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis of a specific scenario in the chapter on manumission for payment (العتق على جعل). The scenario: the master says to his slave 'You are free for a payment' without specifying immediate or future, but it is understood as immediate upon acceptance. However, if the master adds a condition that freedom is tied to payment, the slave becomes a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred until the master's death) but also authorized to trade (مأذون) to earn the payment. The core issue here is whether such a statement that combines an immediate effect (تأثير حال) and a deferred effect (استقبال) is valid. The author of al-Hidayah argues that it is valid because the statement is considered from two aspects: immediate emancipation (إيجاب العتق) and a bequest (وصية). The immediate aspect makes the current slave a mudabbar, while the bequest aspect covers future slaves owned at death. The opponent might object that this combines two contradictory temporal references, but the answer is that they are two different legal acts: one is direct emancipation (which only applies to the present slave), and the other is a bequest (which applies to future acquisitions). Fath al-Qadir critiques this as an Iraqi position, arguing that the combination is not needed; rather, we estimate that at death a separate implicit statement ('every slave I own is free') is made, avoiding any combination in one wording. The practical outcome is that both categories (present slave and later acquisitions) are covered within the third of the estate if they exceed it, and if there is debt, they serve to pay it. The passage concludes by analogizing the case where the master explicitly says 'every slave I own is free when I die', which is treated the same.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A deferred emancipation effective after the master's death, making the slave a mudabbar.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest that takes effect after the testator's death, limited to one-third of the estate.
تقدير (taqdir): Estimating an implicit legal wording to reconcile apparent contradictions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the critique in Fath al-Qadir, how is the combination of immediate and future effects avoided in the case of 'You are free for a payment' tied to payment?
A) By treating the statement as a single act of emancipation only.
B) By estimating an separate implicit phrasing at the time of death.
C) By invalidating the statement and requiring separate wordings.
D) By considering the slave's acceptance as a separate contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a single manumission statement can produce both immediate and deferred effects when analyzed under two distinct legal aspects or by estimating an implicit future wording.

Manumission for Payment: Definition, Acceptance, and Dispute
Defining 'jual' and stating the basic ruling of manumission for payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition and conditions of manumission for payment, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on partial acceptance.

Core Idea
Manumission for payment is an immediate exchange: acceptance triggers freedom, not payment.

This passage opens the chapter on manumission for payment in the Hanafi fiqh text. It defines 'ja'l' as the consideration for freeing a slave and establishes the basic ruling: the slave becomes free immediately upon acceptance, with the payment becoming a debt. The author also details the condition that acceptance must be whole, and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether partial acceptance is valid. Understanding this contract is essential for distinguishing it from kitaba and other conditional manumissions.

Guided Path
1
Define the contract
Identify the linguistic and technical meaning of 'ja'l' and the basic transaction: master offers freedom for a payment, slave accepts.
The payment is called 'ja'l' and can be any type of property.
2
Identify the condition of acceptance
Notice that acceptance is required and must be whole. The text lists various phrasings that all amount to the same legal effect.
Acceptance is immediate; no specific session is required if absent.
3
Analyze the dispute on partial acceptance
Read the discussion: if the slave accepts only half the payment, Abu Hanifah invalidates the contract; the two disciples validate it and the slave becomes free for the full amount. Understand the reasoning: divisibility of manumission.
The dispute hinges on whether manumission is divisible (yutajazza').
4
Determine the scope of valid payment
Note that the payment can be any property (cash, goods, animals, food) as long as the genus is known. Minor ignorance of description is tolerated.
This resembles a sale; hand-to-hand exchange is required, credit is forbidden.

Book of Manumission, Chapter on Manumission for a Payment
It is named after what is given to the mujahid to strengthen him for his jihad. I gave him [ja'altu lahu] means I gave him. And al-ja'il is the plural of ju'ila or ju'ala with vowels, meaning al-ja'l; as in al-Mughrib. What is intended here is manumission for property. His statement: 'He freed his slave for property, and he accepted, he becomes free' i.e., the slave accepts. That is like saying: 'You are free for a thousand', or 'for you to give me a thousand', or 'for you to pay me a thousand', or 'for you to bring me a thousand', or for a debt of a dirham because... or 'for a thousand dirhams that I have against you', or 'for a thousand that you pay me', or he said 'I sold you yourself from you for such-and-such', or 'I granted you yourself on condition that you compensate me such-and-such'. It depends on his acceptance because it is an exchange of property for non-property, since the slave does not own himself. The requirement of exchange is the establishment of the ruling by the acceptance of the consideration immediately, as in a sale. So when he accepts, he becomes free. And it is a condition that a debt is upon him so that suretyship for it is valid, unlike the substitute for kitaba because it is established alongside the contradictory which is the continuation of slavery as known. And just as suretyship is valid with it, it is permissible to exchange it for whatever he wishes hand to hand, so he does not deserve to take possession in the session, so it is permissible to exchange it like silver and gold. There is no good in it on credit because a debt for a debt is forbidden. Acceptance was not restricted to the session because it is known that every acceptance must have a session. If he is present, the session of the offer is considered; if he is absent, his session of knowledge is considered. If he accepts in it, it is valid; if he rejects or turns away, it becomes void. Turning away from it is only by standing up or engaging in another action by which it is known that he has cut what was before him; as in Sharh al-Tahawi. The author did not restrict manumission to payment because he is free before it, since it is not conditioned on payment but is conditioned on acceptance, and that has occurred. And he benefited from his saying 'he accepts' that acceptance must be of the whole. For if he said to his slave 'You are free for a thousand', and he said 'I accept half', then it is not valid according to Abu Hanifah because manumission is divisible according to him. If acceptance of half were permitted, then half of the consideration would be due and the whole would leave his hand because the remainder would go to manumission by service, and the master only consented to the removal of his hand and his being prevented from disposition for a thousand. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid and he becomes free entirely with the entire thousand because manumission is indivisible according to them, so acceptance of half is acceptance of the whole. If that were in divorce, acceptance of half would be acceptance of the whole by agreement, and likewise everything indivisible like blood and other things. If he said to his master 'Free me for a thousand' and he freed half of him, he becomes half-free for nothing; and if it were with the letter ba' (i.e., 'for'), he becomes half-free for five hundred according to the Imam, as in divorce; as in al-Muhit.
And it is restricted to the slave being entirely his. For if he owned half of him and said 'You are free for a thousand' and he accepted, then he becomes half-free for five hundred unless the other co-owner permits, then the thousand is between them according to Abu Hanifah because manumission is divisible according to him, unlike if he said 'I freed my share for a thousand' and the slave accepted, then the thousand is due to the manumitter, the silent partner does not share it because the thousand is in exchange for his share; as in al-Muhit also. The author made the property absolute, so it includes all its types: cash, goods, and animals, even if it is non-specific, because it is exchange of property for non-property, so it resembles marriage, divorce, and reconciliation for intentional homicide, and likewise food, measured, and weighed if the genus is known. Ignorance of description does not harm because it is minor, and he is bound to the middle in naming the animal and the garment after stating their genus.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is the official beginning of the chapter on manumission for a payment (العتق على جعل) in the Hanafi legal text. It first defines the term 'jual' linguistically as something given to a worker for his work, and legally here as the consideration for manumission. The core ruling: a master declares his slave free for a specified payment; the slave must accept for it to take effect immediately. This is a commutative exchange (معاوضة) where the slave receives freedom and the master receives money. The author lists various phrasings that all amount to the same legal effect. The contract is like a sale: immediate upon acceptance. It must be hand-to-hand, no credit, because that would be a debt for a debt. Acceptance must be whole; if the slave accepts half the amount, Abu Hanifah invalidates it because he views manumission as divisible (so half acceptance would imply half freedom for half the amount, but the master did not consent to that). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider manumission indivisible, so partial acceptance is acceptance of the whole. This difference extends to other indivisible matters like divorce and blood money. Another nuance: if the master owns only half the slave, the ruling adjusts accordingly. The payment can be any type of property: cash, goods, livestock, food, etc., as long as the genus is known. Minor ignorance of description is tolerated. This passage sets the foundational rules for this chapter.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'l): The payment or consideration given in exchange for manumission.
عتق على مال (itq 'ala mal): Manumission of a slave in exchange for a payment from the slave.
قبول (qabul): The slave's acceptance of the offer, which is essential for the contract to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if a slave accepts only half of the offered payment in a manumission for payment?
A) The contract is void according to all scholars.
B) The slave becomes half-free for half the payment according to Abu Hanifah.
C) The slave becomes fully free and owes the full amount according to the two disciples.
D) The slave becomes free without any payment.

Key Takeaway
Manumission for payment is a valid contract that frees the slave immediately upon whole acceptance; partial acceptance is valid only if manumission is considered indivisible.

Distinguishing Ta'liq al-'Itq bi al-Ada' from Kitabah
List of 15+ differences between suspended manumission and kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on ta'liq al-'itq bi al-ada' from those on kitabah by identifying at least five practical differences and explaining the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
Ta'liq al-'itq bi al-ada' is a suspension, not a contract — thus the slave is a ma'dhun, not a mukatab.

When a master says to his slave, 'If you pay me a thousand, you are free,' the Hanafi school treats this as a suspended manumission, not a formal kitabah contract. This seemingly small distinction leads to a cascade of practical differences in areas like death of the slave, death of the master, sale of the slave, and the slave's earnings. This lesson unpacks those differences, grounding each in the core legal nature of the transaction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Core Distinction
Identify that ta'liq al-'itq bi al-ada' is a pure suspension (ta'liq) of manumission on a condition, whereas kitabah is a contract of exchange. This foundational difference drives all subsequent rulings.
The slave in ta'liq becomes a ma'dhun (authorized to trade), not a mukatab.
2
Analyze the First Five Differences
Examine differences 1–5: death of slave before payment, death of master, child of female slave, reduction of payment, and payment in different currency. Note how each difference stems from the absence of a debt obligation in ta'liq.
In kitabah, the unpaid installments are a debt on the slave's estate; in ta'liq, they are not.
3
Examine Differences 6–10
Focus on sale/reacquistion of slave (intra-Hanafi dispute), session confinement, permissibility of sale, master's right to take earnings, and surplus after payment. These highlight the master's retained ownership and the slave's restricted rights.
Difference 6 includes an ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
4
Review Differences 11–15 and Additional Cases
Consider the case of pre-existing earnings, payment in different container/color, late payment, payment by third party, and borrowing by slave. These illustrate the strictness of the condition in ta'liq.
The rule of session confinement applies only with the particle 'in'; with 'idha' or 'mata', it is not confined.
5
Appreciate the Role of the Response Particle
Note that the validity of the ta'liq depends on the response being connected with the particle 'fa'. If 'waw' is used or the response is not connected, the manumission takes effect immediately (tanjiz).
This is a key usul point highlighted at the end of the passage.

قوله : (ولو علق عتقه بادائه صار مأذوناً) أي بأداء المال كأن يقول إن أديت إلي ألفاً حر فيصح ويعتق عند الأداء من غير أن يصير مكاتباً لأنه صريح في تعليق العتق بالأداء وإن كان فيه معنى المعاوضة في الانتهاء وإنما صار مأذوناً لأنه رغبة في الاكتساب لطلبه الأداء منه ومراده التجارة دون التكدي فكان إذناً له دلالة وذكر في فتح القدير أنه يخالف المكاتب في إحدى عشرة مسألة : الأولى ما إذا مات العبد قبل الأداء وترك مالاً فهو للمولى ولا يؤدي منه عنه ويعتق بخلاف الكتابة الثانية لو مات المولى وفي يد العبد كسب كان لورثة المولى ويباع العبد بخلاف الكتابة الثالثة لو كانت أمة فولدت ثم أدت فعتقت لم يعتق ولدها
